10046{_}3904{_}Presently food was ready, and our rude meal in that darkling place was a merry one.
10046{_}3905{_}Elspeth sat enthroned on a couch of pine branches--I can see her yet shielding her face from the blaze with one little hand, and dividing her cakes with the other.
10046{_}3906{_}Then we lit our pipes, and fell to the long tales of the camp-fire.
10046{_}3907{_}Ringan had a story of a black-haired princess of Spain, and how for love of her two gentlemen did marvels on the seas.
10046{_}3908{_}The chief one never returned to claim her, but died in a fight off Cartagena, and wrote a fine ballad about his mistress which Ringan said was still sung in the taverns of the Main.
10046{_}3909{_}He gave a verse of it, a wild, sad thing, with tears in it and the joy of battle.
10046{_}3910{_}After that we all sang, all but me, who have no voice.
10046{_}3911{_}Bertrand had a lay of Normandy, about a lady who walked in the apple-orchards and fell in love with a wandering minstrel; and Donaldson sang a rough ballad of Virginia, in which a man weighs the worth of his wife against a tankard of apple-jack.
10046{_}3912{_}Grey sang an English song about the north-country maid who came to London, and a bit of the chanty of the Devon men who sacked Santa Fe and stole the Almirante's daughter.
10046{_}3913{_}As for Elspeth, she sang to a soft Scots tune the tale of the Lady of Cassilis who followed the gipsy's piping.
10046{_}3914{_}In it the gipsy tells of what he can offer the lady, and lo!
10046{_}3915{_}it was our own case!--       "And ye shall wear no silken gown,         No maid shall bind your hair;       The yellow broom shall be your gem,         Your braid the heather rare.
10046{_}3916{_}"Athwart the moor, adown the hill,         Across the world away!
10046{_}3917{_}The path is long for happy hearts         That sing to greet the day,                    My love,         That sing to greet the day."
10046{_}3918{_}I remember, too, the last verse of it:--       "And at the last no solemn stole         Shall on thy breast be laid;       No mumbling priest shall speed thy soul,         No charnel vault thee shade.
10046{_}3919{_}But by the shadowed hazel copse,         Aneath the greenwood tree,       Where airs are soft and waters sing,         Thou'lt ever sleep by me,                 My love,         Thou'lt ever sleep by me."
10046{_}3920{_}Then we fell to talking about the things in the West that no man had yet discovered, and Shalah, to whom our songs were nothing, now lent an ear.
10046{_}3921{_}"The first Virginians," said Grey, "thought that over the hills lay the western ocean and the road to Cathay.
10046{_}3922{_}I do not know, but I am confident that but a little way west we should come to water.
10046{_}3923{_}A great river or else the ocean."
10046{_}3924{_}Ringan differed.
10046{_}3925{_}He held that the land of America was very wide in those parts, as wide as south of the isthmus where no man had yet crossed it.
10046{_}3926{_}Then he told us of a sea-captain who had travelled inland in Mexico for five weeks and come to a land where gold was as common as chuckiestones, and a great people dwelt who worshipped a god who lived in a mountain.
10046{_}3927{_}And he spoke of the holy city of Manoa, which Sir Walter Raleigh sought, and which many had seen from far hill-tops.
10046{_}3928{_}Likewise of the wonderful kings who once dwelt in Peru, and the little isle in the Pacific where all the birds were nightingales and the Tree of Life flourished; and the mountain north of the Main which was all one emerald.
10046{_}3929{_}"I think," he said, "that, though no man has ever had the fruition of these marvels, they are likely to be more true than false.
10046{_}3930{_}I hold that God has kept this land of America to the last to be the loadstone of adventurers, and that there are greater wonders to be seen than any that man has imagined.
10046{_}3931{_}The pity is that I have spent my best years scratching like a hen at its doorstep instead of entering.
10046{_}3932{_}I have a notion some day to travel straight west to the sunset.
10046{_}3933{_}I think I should find death, but I might see some queer things first."
10046{_}3934{_}Then Shalah spoke:--  "There was once a man of my own people who, when he came to man's strength, journeyed westward with a wife.
10046{_}3935{_}He travelled all his days, and when his eyes were dim with age he saw a great water.
10046{_}3936{_}His spirit left him on its shore, but on his road he had begotten a son, and that son journeyed back towards the rising sun, and came after many years to his people again.
10046{_}3937{_}I have spoken with him of what he had seen."
10046{_}3938{_}"And what was that?"
10046{_}3939{_}asked Ringan, with eager eyes.
10046{_}3940{_}"He told of plains so great that it is a lifetime to travel over them, and of deserts where the eagle flying from the dawn dies of drought by midday, and of mountains so high that birds cannot cross them but are changed by cold into stone, and of rivers to which our little waters are as reeds to a forest cedar.
10046{_}3941{_}But especially he spoke of the fierce warriors that ride like the wind on horses.
10046{_}3942{_}It seems, brother, that he who would reach that land must reach also the Hereafter."
10046{_}3943{_}"That's the place for me," Ringan cried.
10046{_}3944{_}"What say you, Andrew?
10046{_}3945{_}When this affair is over, shall we make a bid for these marvels?
10046{_}3946{_}I can cull some pretty adventurers from the Free Companions."
10046{_}3947{_}"Nay, I am for moving a step at a time," said I.
10046{_}3948{_}"I am a trader, and want one venture well done before I begin on another, I shall be content if we safely cross these mountains on which we are now perched."
10046{_}3949{_}Ringan shook his head.
10046{_}3950{_}"That was never the way of the Highlands, 'Better a bone on the far-away hills than a fat sheep in the meadows,' says the Gael.
10046{_}3951{_}What say you, mistress?"
10046{_}3952{_}and he turned to Elspeth.
10046{_}3953{_}"I think you are the born poet," said she, smiling, "and that Mr. Garvald is the sober man of affairs.
10046{_}3954{_}You will leap for the top of the wall and get a prospect while Mr. Garvald will patiently pull it down."
10046{_}3955{_}"Oh, I grant that Andrew has the wisdom," said Ringan.
10046{_}3956{_}"That's why him and me's so well agreed.
10046{_}3957{_}It's because we differ much, and so fit together like opposite halves of an apple.... Is your traveller still in the land of the living?"
10046{_}3958{_}he asked Shalah.
10046{_}3959{_}But the Indian had slipped away from the fireside circle, and I saw him without in the moonlight standing rigid on a knoll and gazing at the skies.
10046{_}3960{_}*       *       *       *       *  Next day dawned cloudless, and Shalah and I spent it in a long journey along the range.
10046{_}3961{_}We kept to the highest parts, and at every vantage-ground we scanned the glens for human traces.
10046{_}3962{_}By this time I had found my hill legs, and could keep pace even with the Indian's swift stride.
10046{_}3963{_}The ridge of mountains, you must know, was not a single backbone, but broken up here and there by valleys into two and even three ranges.
10046{_}3964{_}This made our scouting more laborious, and prevented us from getting the full value out of our high station.
10046{_}3965{_}Mostly we kept in cover, and never showed on a skyline.
10046{_}3966{_}But we saw nothing to prove the need of this stealth.
10046{_}3967{_}Only the hawks wheeled, and the wild pigeons crooned; the squirrels frisked among the branches; and now and then a great deer would leap from its couch and hasten into the coverts.
10046{_}3968{_}But, though we got no news, that journey brought to me a revelation, for I had my glimpse of Studd's Promised Land.
10046{_}3969{_}It came to me early in the day, as we halted in a little glade, gay with willowherb and goldenrod, which hung on a shelf of the hills looking westwards.
10046{_}3970{_}The first streamers of morn had gone, the mists had dried up from the valleys, and I found myself looking into a deep cleft and across at a steep pine-clad mountain.
10046{_}3971{_}Clearly the valley was split by this mountain into two forks, and I could see only the cool depth of it and catch a gleam of broken water a mile or two below.
10046{_}3972{_}But looking more to the north, I saw where the vale opened, and then I had a vision worthy of the name by which Studd had baptized it.
10046{_}3973{_}An immense green pasture land ran out to the dim horizon.
10046{_}3974{_}There were forests scattered athwart it, and single great trees, and little ridges, too, but at the height where we stood it seemed to the eye to be one verdant meadow as trim and shapely as the lawn of a garden.
10046{_}3975{_}A noble river, the child of many hill streams, twined through it in shining links.
10046{_}3976{_}I could see dots, which I took to be herds of wild cattle grazing, but no sign of any human dweller.
10046{_}3977{_}"What is it?"
10046{_}3978{_}I asked unthinkingly.
10046{_}3979{_}"The Shenandoah," Shalah said, and I never stopped to ask how he knew the name.
10046{_}3980{_}He was gazing at the sight with hungry eyes, he whose gaze was, for usual, so passionless.
10046{_}3981{_}That prospect gave me a happy feeling of comfort; why, I cannot tell, except that the place looked so bright and habitable.
10046{_}3982{_}Here was no sour wilderness, but a land made by God for cheerful human dwellings.
10046{_}3983{_}Some day there would be orchards and gardens among those meadows, and miles of golden corn, and the smoke of hearth fires.
10046{_}3984{_}Some day I would enter into that land of Canaan which now I saw from Pisgah.
10046{_}3985{_}Some day--and I scarcely dared the thought--my children would call it home.
10046{_}3986{_}CHAPTER XXI.
10046{_}3987{_}A HAWK SCREAMS IN THE EVENING  Those two days in the stockade were like a rift of sun in a stormy day, and the next morn the clouds descended.
10046{_}3988{_}The face of nature seemed to be a mirror of our fortunes, for when I woke the freshness had gone out of the air, and in the overcast sky there was a forewarning of storm.
10046{_}3989{_}But the little party in the camp remained cheerful enough.
10046{_}3990{_}Donaldson and Bertrand went off to their trapping; Elspeth was braiding her hair, the handsomest nymph that ever trod these woodlands, and trying in vain to discover from the discreet Ringan where he came from, and what was his calling.
10046{_}3991{_}The two Borderers knew well who he was; Grey, I think, had a suspicion; but it never entered the girl's head that this debonair gentleman bore the best known name in all the Americas.
10046{_}3992{_}She fancied he was some exiled Jacobite, and was ready to hear a pitiful romance.
10046{_}3993{_}This at another time she would have readily got; but Ringan for the nonce was in a sober mood, and though he would talk of Breadalbane, was chary of touching on more recent episodes.
10046{_}3994{_}All she learned was that he was a great traveller, and had tried most callings that merit a gentleman's interest.
10046{_}3995{_}The day before, Shalah and I had explored the range to the south, keeping on the west side where we thought the enemy were likely to gather.
10046{_}3996{_}This day we looked to the side facing the Tidewater, a difficult job, for it was eaten into by the upper glens of many rivers.
10046{_}3997{_}The weather grew hot and oppressive, and over the lowlands of Virginia there brooded a sullen thundercloud.
10046{_}3998{_}It oppressed my spirits, and I found myself less able to keep up with Shalah.
10046{_}3999{_}The constant sight of the lowlands filled me with anxiety for what might be happening in those sullen blue flats.
10046{_}4000{_}Gone was the glad forgetfulness of yesterday.
10046{_}4001{_}The Promised Land might smile as it pleased, but we were still on the flanks of Pisgah with the Midianites all about us.
10046{_}4002{_}My recollection of that day is one of heavy fatigue and a pressing hopelessness.
10046{_}4003{_}Shalah behaved oddly, for he was as restive as a frightened stag.
10046{_}4004{_}No covert was unsuspected by him, and if I ventured to raise my head on any exposed ground a long brown arm pulled me down.
10046{_}4005{_}He would make no answer to my questions except a grunt.
10046{_}4006{_}All this gave me the notion that the hills were full of the enemy, and I grew as restive as the Indian.
10046{_}4007{_}The crackle of a branch startled me, and the movement of a scared beast brought my heart to my throat.
10046{_}4008{_}Then from a high place he saw something which sent us both crawling into the thicket.
10046{_}4009{_}We made a circuit of several miles round the head of a long ravine, and came to a steep bank of red screes.
10046{_}4010{_}Up this we wormed our way, as flat as snakes, with our noses in the dusty earth.
10046{_}4011{_}I was dripping with sweat, and cursing to myself this new madness of Shalah's.
10046{_}4012{_}Then I found a cooler air blowing on the top of my prostrate skull, and I judged that we were approaching the scarp of a ridge.
10046{_}4013{_}Shalah's hand held me motionless.
10046{_}4014{_}He wriggled on a little farther, and with immense slowness raised his head.
10046{_}4015{_}His hand now beckoned me forward, and in a few seconds I was beside him and was lifting my eyes over the edge of the scarp.
10046{_}4016{_}Below us lay a little plain, wedged in between two mountains, and breaking off on one side into a steep glen.
10046{_}4017{_}It was just such a shelf as I had seen in the Carolinas, only a hundred times greater, and it lay some five hundred feet below us.
10046{_}4018{_}Every part of the hollow was filled with men.
10046{_}4019{_}Thousands there must have been, around their fires and teepees, and coming or going from the valley.
10046{_}4020{_}They were silent, like all savages, but the low hum rose from the place which told of human life.
10046{_}4021{_}I tried to keep my eyes steady, though my heart was beating like a fanner.
10046{_}4022{_}The men were of the same light colour and slimness as those I had seen on the edge of the mist in Clearwater Glen.
10046{_}4023{_}Indeed, they were not unlike Shalah, except that he was bigger than the most of them.
10046{_}4024{_}I was not learned in Indian ways, but a glance told me that these folk never came out of the Tidewater, and were no Cherokees of the hills or Tuscaroras from the Carolinas.
10046{_}4025{_}They were a new race from the west or the north, the new race which had so long been perplexing us.
10046{_}4026{_}Somewhere among them was the brain which had planned for the Tidewater a sudden destruction.
10046{_}4027{_}Shalah slipped noiselessly backward, and I followed him down the scree slope, across the ravine, and then with infinite caution through the sparse woods till we had put a wide shoulder of hill between us and the enemy.
10046{_}4028{_}After that we started running, such a pace as made the rush back to the Rappahannock seem an easy saunter.
10046{_}4029{_}Shalah would avoid short-cuts for no reason that I could see, and make long circuits in places where I had to go on hands and feet.
10046{_}4030{_}I was weary before we set out, and soon I began to totter like a drunken man.
10046{_}4031{_}The Indian's arm pulled me up countless times, and his face, usually so calm, was now sharp with care.
10046{_}4032{_}"You cannot fail here, brother," he would say, "On our speed hang the lives of all."
10046{_}4033{_}That put me on my mettle, for it was Elspeth's safety I now strove for, and the thought gave life to my leaden limbs.
10046{_}4034{_}Every minute the air grew heavier, and the sky darker, so that when about five in the afternoon we passed the Gap and struggled up the last hill to the stockade, it seemed as if night had already fallen.
10046{_}4035{_}Elspeth and Ringan were there, and the two trappers had just returned.
10046{_}4036{_}I could do nothing but pant on the ground, but Shalah cried out for news of Grey.
10046{_}4037{_}He heard that he had gone into the woods with his musket two hours past.
10046{_}4038{_}At this he flung up his hands with a motion of despair.
10046{_}4039{_}"We cannot wait," he said to Ringan.
10046{_}4040{_}"Close the gate and put every man to his post, for the danger is at hand."
10046{_}4041{_}Ringan gave his orders.
10046{_}4042{_}The big log gate was barred, the fire trampled out, and we waited in that thunderous darkness.
10046{_}4043{_}A long draught of cold water had revived me, and I could think clearly of Elspeth.
10046{_}4044{_}Her bower was in the safest part of the stockade, but she would not stay there, I could see terror in her eyes, but she gave no sign of it.
10046{_}4045{_}She made ready our supper of cold meat as if she had no other thought in the world.
10046{_}4046{_}Waiting on an attack is a hard trial for mortal nerves.
10046{_}4047{_}I am not ashamed to confess that in those minutes my courage was little to boast of.
10046{_}4048{_}I envied Ringan his ease, and Bertrand his light cheerfulness, and Donaldson his unshaken gravity, and especially I envied Shalah his godlike calm.
10046{_}4049{_}But most of all I envied Elspeth the courage which could know desperate fear and never show it.
10046{_}4050{_}Most likely I did myself some wrong.
10046{_}4051{_}Most likely my own face was firm enough, but, if it were, 'twas a poor clue to the brain behind it.
10046{_}4052{_}I fell to wondering about Grey still travelling in the woods.
10046{_}4053{_}Was there any hope for him?
10046{_}4054{_}Was there hope, indeed, for any one of us penned in a wooden palisade fifty miles from aid, a handful against an army?
10046{_}4055{_}Presently in the lowering silence came the scream of a hawk.
10046{_}4056{_}An uncommon sound, half croak, half cry, which only hill dwellers know, but 'tis an eery noise in the wilderness.
10046{_}4057{_}It came again, less near, and a third time from a great distance.
10046{_}4058{_}I thought it queer, for a hawk does not scream twice in the same hour.
10046{_}4059{_}I looked at Shalah, who stood by the gate, every sinew in his body taut with expectation.
10046{_}4060{_}He caught my eye.
10046{_}4061{_}"That hawk never flew on wings," he said.
10046{_}4062{_}Then an owl hooted, and from near at hand came the cough of a deer.
10046{_}4063{_}The thicket was alive with life, which mimicked the wild things of the woods.
10046{_}4064{_}Then came a sound which drowned all others.
10046{_}4065{_}From the inky sky descended a jagged line of light, and in the same second the crash of the thunder broke.
10046{_}4066{_}Never have I seen such a storm.
10046{_}4067{_}Down in the Tidewater we had thunderstorms in plenty during the summer-time, but they growled and passed and scarce ruffled the even blue of the sky.
10046{_}4068{_}But here it looked as if we had found the home of the lightnings, where all the thunderbolts were forged.
10046{_}4069{_}It blazed around us like a steady fire.
10046{_}4070{_}By a miracle the palisade was not struck, but I heard a rending and splintering in the forest where tall trees had met their doom.
10046{_}4071{_}The noise deafened me, and confused my senses.
10046{_}4072{_}Out of the loophole I could see the glade that sloped down to the Gap, and it was as bright as if it had been high noonday.
10046{_}4073{_}The clumps of fern and grass stood out yellow and staring against the inky background of the trees.
10046{_}4074{_}I remember I noted a rabbit run confusedly into the open, and then at a fresh flare of lightning scamper back.
10046{_}4075{_}Something was crouching and shivering at my side.
10046{_}4076{_}I found it was Elspeth, whose courage was no match for the terrors of the heavens.
10046{_}4077{_}She snuggled against me for companionship, and hid her face in the sleeve of my coat.
10046{_}4078{_}Suddenly came a cry from Shalah on my left.
10046{_}4079{_}He pointed his hand to the glade, and in it I saw a man running.
10046{_}4080{_}A new burst of light sprang up, for some dry tindery creepers had caught fire, and were blazing to heaven.
10046{_}4081{_}It lit a stumbling figure which I saw was Grey, and behind him was a lithe Indian running on his trail.
10046{_}4082{_}"Open the gate," I cried, and I got my musket in the loophole.
10046{_}4083{_}The fugitive was all but spent.
10046{_}4084{_}He ran, bowed almost to the ground, with a wild back glance ever and again over his shoulder.
10046{_}4085{_}His pursuer gained on him with great strides, and in his hand he carried a bare knife.
10046{_}4086{_}I dared not shoot, for Grey was between me and his enemy.
10046{_}4087{_}'Twas as well I could not, for otherwise Grey would never have reached us alive.
10046{_}4088{_}We cried to him to swerve, and the sound of our voices brought up that last flicker of hope which waits till the end in every man.
10046{_}4089{_}He seemed actually to gain a yard, and now he was near enough for us to see his white face and staring eyes.
10046{_}4090{_}Then he stumbled, and the man with the knife was almost on him.
10046{_}4091{_}But he found his feet again, and swerved like a hunted hare in one desperate bound.
10046{_}4092{_}This gave me my chance: my musket cracked, and the Indian pitched quietly to the ground.
10046{_}4093{_}The knife flew out of his hand and almost touched Grey's heel.
10046{_}4094{_}With the sound Shalah had leaped from the gate, picked up Grey like a child, and in a second had him inside the palisade and the bars down.
10046{_}4095{_}He was none too soon, for as his pursuer fell a flight of arrows broke from the thicket, and had I shot earlier Grey had died of them.
10046{_}4096{_}As it was they were too late.
10046{_}4097{_}The bowmen rushed into the glade, and five muskets from our side took toll of them.
10046{_}4098{_}My last vision was of leaping yellow devils capering from among blazing trees.
10046{_}4099{_}Then without warning it was dark again, and from the skies fell a deluge of rain.
10046{_}4100{_}In a minute the burning creepers were quenched, and the whole world was one pit of ink, with the roar as of a thousand torrents about our ears.
10046{_}4101{_}As the vividness of the lightning, so was the weight of the rain.
10046{_}4102{_}Ringan cried to us to stand to our places, for now was the likely occasion for attack; but no human being could have fought in such weather.
10046{_}4103{_}Indeed, we could not hear him, and he had to stagger round and shout his command into each several ear.
10046{_}4104{_}The might of the deluge almost pressed me to the earth, I carried Elspeth into her bower, but the roof of branches was speedily beaten down, and it was no better than a peat bog.
10046{_}4105{_}That overwhelming storm lasted for maybe a quarter of an hour, and then it stopped as suddenly as it came.
10046{_}4106{_}Inside the palisade the ground swam like a loch, and from the hill-side came the rumour of a thousand swollen streams.
10046{_}4107{_}That, with the heavy drip of laden branches, made sound enough, but after the thunder and the downpour it seemed silence itself.
10046{_}4108{_}Presently when I looked up I saw that the black wrack was clearing from the sky, and through a gap there shone a watery star.
10046{_}4109{_}Ringan took stock of our defences, and doled out to each a portion of sodden meat.
10046{_}4110{_}Grey had found his breath by this time, and had got a spare musket, for his own had been left in the woods.
10046{_}4111{_}Elspeth had had her wits sorely jangled by the storm, and in the revulsion was on the brink of tears.
10046{_}4112{_}She was very tender towards Grey's condition, and the sight gave me no jealousy, for in that tense hour all things were forgotten but life and death.
10046{_}4113{_}Donaldson, at Ringan's bidding, saw to the feeding of the horses as if he were in his own stable on the Rappahannock.
10046{_}4114{_}It takes all sorts of men to make a world, but I thought at the time that for this business the steel nerves of the Borderer were worth many quicker brains and more alert spirits.
10046{_}4115{_}The hours marched sombrely towards midnight, while we stayed every man by his post.
10046{_}4116{_}I asked Shalah if the enemy had gone, and he shook his head.
10046{_}4117{_}He had the sense of a wild animal to detect danger in the forest when the eye and ear gave no proof.
10046{_}4118{_}He stood like a stag, sniffing the night air, and peering with his deep eyes into the gloom.
10046{_}4119{_}Fortunately, though the moon was all but full, the sky was so overcast that only the faintest yellow glow broke into the darkness of the hill-tops.
10046{_}4120{_}It must have been an hour after midnight when we got our next warning of the enemy.
10046{_}4121{_}Suddenly a firebrand leaped from farther up the hill, and flew in a wide curve into the middle of the stockade.
10046{_}4122{_}It fell on the partition between the horses and ourselves and hung crackling there.
10046{_}4123{_}A shower of arrows followed it, which missed us, for we were close to the edges of the palisade.
10046{_}4124{_}But the sputtering torch was a danger, for presently it would show our position; so Bertrand very gallantly pulled it down, stamped it out, and got back to his post unscathed.
10046{_}4125{_}Yet the firebrand had done its work, for it had showed the savages where the horses stood picketed.
10046{_}4126{_}Another followed, lighting in their very midst, and setting them plunging at their ropes.
10046{_}4127{_}I heard Ringan curse deeply, for we had not thought of this stratagem.
10046{_}4128{_}And the next second I became aware that there was some one among the horses.
10046{_}4129{_}At first I thought that the palisade had been stormed, and then I heard a soft voice which was no Indian's.
10046{_}4130{_}Heedless of orders, I flung myself at the rough gate, and in a trice was beside the voice.
10046{_}4131{_}Elspeth was busy among the startled beasts.
10046{_}4132{_}She had a passion for horses, and had, as we say, the "cool" hand with them, for she would soothe a frightened stallion by rubbing his nose and whispering in his ear.
10046{_}4133{_}By the time I got to her she had stamped out the torch, and was stroking Grey's mare, which was the worst scared.
10046{_}4134{_}Her own fear had gone, and in that place of plunging hooves and tossing manes she was as calm as in a summer garden.
10046{_}4135{_}"Let me be, Andrew," she said.
10046{_}4136{_}"I am better at this business than you."
10046{_}4137{_}She had the courage of a lion, but 'twas a wild courage, without foresight.
10046{_}4138{_}Another firebrand came circling through the darkness, and broke on the head of Donaldson's pony.
10046{_}4139{_}I caught the girl and swung her off her feet into safety.
10046{_}4140{_}And then on the heels of the torch came a flight of arrows, fired from near at hand.
10046{_}4141{_}By the mercy of God she was unharmed.
10046{_}4142{_}I had one through the sleeve of my coat, but none reached her.
10046{_}4143{_}One took a horse in the neck, and the poor creature screamed pitifully.
10046{_}4144{_}Presently there was a wild confusion of maddened beasts, with the torch burning on the ground and lighting the whole place for the enemy.
10046{_}4145{_}I had Elspeth in my arms, and was carrying her to the gate, when over the palisade I saw yellow limbs and fierce faces.
10046{_}4146{_}They saw it too--Ringan and the rest--and it did not need his cry to keep our posts to tell us the right course.
10046{_}4147{_}The inner palisade which shut off the horses must now be our line of defence, and the poor beasts must be left to their fate.
10046{_}4148{_}But Elspeth and I had still to get inside it.
10046{_}4149{_}Her ankle had caught in a picket rope, which in another second would have wrenched it cruelly, had I not slashed it free with my knife.
10046{_}4150{_}This sent the horse belonging to it in wild career across the corral, and I think 'twas that interruption which saved our lives.
10046{_}4151{_}It held back the savages for an instant of time, and prevented them blocking our escape.
10046{_}4152{_}It all took place in the flutter of an eye-lid, though it takes long in the telling.
10046{_}4153{_}I pushed Elspeth through the door, and with all my strength tore at the bars.
10046{_}4154{_}But they would not move.
10046{_}4155{_}Perhaps the rain had swollen the logs, and they had jammed too tightly to let the bar slide in the groove.
10046{_}4156{_}So I found myself in that gate, the mad horses and the savages before me, and my friends at my back, with only my arm to hold the post.
10046{_}4157{_}I had my musket and my two pistols--three shots, for there would be no time to reload.
10046{_}4158{_}A yellow shadow slipped below a horse's belly, and there came the cry of an animal's agony.
10046{_}4159{_}Then another and another, and yet more.
10046{_}4160{_}But no one came near me in the gateway.
10046{_}4161{_}I could not see anything to shoot at--only lithe shades and mottled shadows, for the torch lay on the wet ground, and was sputtering to its end.
10046{_}4162{_}The moaning of the horses maddened me, and I sent a bullet through the head of my own poor beast, which was writhing horribly.
10046{_}4163{_}Elspeth's horse got the contents of my second pistol.
10046{_}4164{_}And then it seemed that the raiders had gone.
10046{_}4165{_}There was one bit of the far palisade which was outlined for me dimly against a gap in the trees.
10046{_}4166{_}I saw a figure on it, and whipped my musket to my shoulder.
10046{_}4167{_}Something flung up its arms and toppled back among the dying beasts.
10046{_}4168{_}Then a hand--Donaldson's, I think--clutched me and pulled me back.
10046{_}4169{_}With a great effort the bars were brought down, and I found myself beside Elspeth.
10046{_}4170{_}All her fortitude had gone now, and she was sobbing like a child.
10046{_}4171{_}Gradually the moaning of the horses ceased, and the whole world seemed cold and silent as a stone.
10046{_}4172{_}We stood our watch till a wan sunrise struggled up the hill-side.
10046{_}4173{_}CHAPTER XXII.
10046{_}4174{_}HOW A FOOL MUST GO HIS OWN ROAD.
10046{_}4175{_}It was a sorry party that looked at each other in the first light of dawn.
10046{_}4176{_}Our eyes were hollow with suspense, and all but Shalah had the hunted look of men caught in a trap.
10046{_}4177{_}Not till the sun had got above the tree-tops did we venture to leave our posts and think of food.
10046{_}4178{_}It was now that Elspeth's spirit showed supreme.
10046{_}4179{_}The courage of that pale girl put us all to the blush.
10046{_}4180{_}She alone carried her head high and forced an air of cheerfulness.
10046{_}4181{_}She lit the fire with Donaldson's help, and broiled some deer's flesh for our breakfast, and whistled gently as she wrought, bringing into our wild business a breath of the orderly comfort of home.
10046{_}4182{_}I had seen her in silk and lace, a queen among the gallants, but she never looked so fair as on that misty morning, her hair straying over her brow, her plain kirtle soiled and sodden, but her eyes bright with her young courage.
10046{_}4183{_}During the last hours of that dark vigil my mind had been torn with cares.
10046{_}4184{_}If we escaped the perils of the night, I asked myself, what then?
10046{_}4185{_}Here were the seven of us, pinned in a hill-fort, with no help within fifty miles, and one of the seven was a woman!
10046{_}4186{_}I judged that the Indian force was large, and there was always the mighty army waiting farther south in that shelf of the hills.
10046{_}4187{_}If they sought to take us, it must be a matter of a day or two at the most till they succeeded.
10046{_}4188{_}If they only played with us--which is the cruel Indian way--we might resist a little, but starvation would beat us down.
10046{_}4189{_}Where were we to get food, with the forests full of our subtle enemies?
10046{_}4190{_}To sit still would mean to wait upon death, and the waiting would not be long.
10046{_}4191{_}There was the chance, to be sure, that the Indians would be drawn off in the advance towards the east.
10046{_}4192{_}But here came in a worse anxiety.
10046{_}4193{_}I had come to get news to warn the Tidewater.
10046{_}4194{_}That news I had got.
10046{_}4195{_}The mighty gathering which Shalah's eyes and mine had beheld in that upland glen was the peril we had foreseen.
10046{_}4196{_}What good were easy victories over raiding Cherokees when this deadly host waited on the leash?
10046{_}4197{_}I had no doubt that the Cherokees were now broken.
10046{_}4198{_}Stafford county would be full of Nicholson's militia, and Lawrence's strong hand lay on the line of the Borders.
10046{_}4199{_}But what availed it?
10046{_}4200{_}While Virginia was flattering herself that she had repelled the savages, and the Rappahannock men were notching their muskets with the tale of the dead, a wave was gathering to sweep down the Pamunkey or the James, and break on the walls of James Town.
10046{_}4201{_}I did not think that Nicholson, forewarned and prepared, could stem the torrent; and if it caught him unawares the proud Tidewater would break like a rotten reed.
10046{_}4202{_}I had been sent to scout.
10046{_}4203{_}Was I to be false to the word I had given, and let any risk to myself or others deter me from taking back the news?
10046{_}4204{_}The Indian army tarried; why, I did not know--perhaps some mad whim of their soothsayers, perhaps the device of a wise general; but at any rate they tarried.
10046{_}4205{_}If a war party could spend a night in baiting us and slaying our horses, there could be no very instant orders for the road.
10046{_}4206{_}If this were so, a bold man might yet reach the Border line.
10046{_}4207{_}At that moment it seemed to me a madman's errand.
10046{_}4208{_}Even if I slipped past the watchers in the woods and the glens, the land between would be strewn with fragments of the Cherokee host, and I had not the Indian craft.
10046{_}4209{_}But it was very seriously borne in upon me that 'twas my duty to try.
10046{_}4210{_}God might prosper a bold stroke, and in any case I should be true to my trust.
10046{_}4211{_}But what of Elspeth?
10046{_}4212{_}The thought of leaving her was pure torment.
10046{_}4213{_}In our hideous peril 'twas scarcely to be endured that one should go.
10046{_}4214{_}I told myself that if I reached the Border I could get help, but my heart warned me that I lied.
10046{_}4215{_}My news would leave no time there for riding hillward to rescue a rash adventure.
10046{_}4216{_}We were beyond the pale, and must face the consequences.
10046{_}4217{_}That we all had known, and reckoned with, but we had not counted that our risk would be shared by a woman.
10046{_}4218{_}Ah I that luckless ride of Elspeth's!
10046{_}4219{_}But for that foolish whim she would be safe now in the cool house at Middle Plantation, with a ship to take her to safety if the worst befell.
10046{_}4220{_}And now of all the King's subjects in that hour we were the most ill-fated, islanded on a sand heap with the tide of savage war hourly eating into our crazy shelter.
10046{_}4221{_}Before the daylight came, as I stood with my cheek to my musket, I had come to a resolution.
10046{_}4222{_}In a tangle of duties a man must seize the solitary clear one, and there could be no doubt of what mine was, I must try for the Tidewater, and I must try alone, Shalah had the best chance to get through, but without Shalah the stockade was no sort of refuge.
10046{_}4223{_}Ringan was wiser and stronger than I, but I thought I had more hill-craft, and, besides, the duty was mine, not his.
10046{_}4224{_}Grey had no knowledge of the wilds, and Donaldson and Bertrand could not handle the news as it should be handled, in the unlikely event of their getting through alive.
10046{_}4225{_}No, there were no two ways of it.
10046{_}4226{_}I must make the effort, though in that leaden hour of weariness and cold it seemed as if my death-knell were ringing.
10046{_}4227{_}Morn showed a grey world, strewn with the havoc of the storm.
10046{_}4228{_}The eagles were already busy among the dead horses, and our first job was to bury the poor beasts.
10046{_}4229{_}Just outside the stockade we dug as best we could a shallow trench, while the muskets of the others kept watch over us.
10046{_}4230{_}There we laid also the body of the man I had shot in the night.
10046{_}4231{_}He was a young savage, naked to the waist, and curiously tattooed on the forehead with the device of what seemed to be a rising or setting sun.
10046{_}4232{_}I observed that Shalah looked closely at this, and that his face wore an unusual excitement.
10046{_}4233{_}He said something in his own tongue, and, when the trench was dug, laid the dead man in it so that his head pointed westwards.
10046{_}4234{_}We wrought in a dogged silence, and Elspeth's cheery whistling was the only sound in that sullen morning.
10046{_}4235{_}It fairly broke my heart.
10046{_}4236{_}She was whistling the old tune of "Leezie Lindsay," a merry lilt with the hill wind and the heather in it.
10046{_}4237{_}The bravery of the poor child was the hardest thing of all to bear when I knew that in a few hours' time the end might come.
10046{_}4238{_}The others were only weary and dishevelled and ill at ease, but on me seemed to have fallen the burden of the cares of the whole earth.
10046{_}4239{_}Shalah had disappeared for a little, and came back with the word that the near forests were empty.
10046{_}4240{_}So I summoned a council, and talked as we breakfasted.
10046{_}4241{_}I had looked into the matter of the food, and found that we had sufficient for three days.
10046{_}4242{_}We had boucanned a quantity of deer's flesh two days before, and this, with the fruit of yesterday's trapping, made a fair stock in our larder.
10046{_}4243{_}Then I announced my plan.
10046{_}4244{_}"I am going to try to reach Lawrence," I said.
10046{_}4245{_}No one spoke.
10046{_}4246{_}Shalah lifted his head, and looked at me gravely.
10046{_}4247{_}"Does any man object?"
10046{_}4248{_}I asked sharply, for my temper was all of an edge.
10046{_}4249{_}"Your throat will be cut in the first mile," said Donaldson gruffly.
10046{_}4250{_}"Maybe it will, but maybe not.
10046{_}4251{_}At any rate, I can try.
10046{_}4252{_}You have not heard what Shalah and I found in the hills yesterday.
10046{_}4253{_}Twelve miles south there is a glen with a plateau at its head, and that plateau is as full of Indians as a beehive.
10046{_}4254{_}Ay, Ringan, you and Lawrence were right.
10046{_}4255{_}The Cherokees are the least of the trouble.
10046{_}4256{_}There's a great army come out of the West, men that you and I never saw the like of before, and they are waiting till the Cherokees have drawn the fire of the Borderers, and then they will bring hell to the Tidewater.
10046{_}4257{_}You and I know that there's some sort of madman in command, a man that quotes the Bible and speaks English; but madman or not, he's a great general, and woe betide Virginia if he gets among the manors.
10046{_}4258{_}I was sent to the hills to get news, and I've got it.
10046{_}4259{_}Would it not be the part of a coward to bide here and make no effort to warn our friends?"
10046{_}4260{_}"What good would a warning do?"
10046{_}4261{_}said Ringan.
10046{_}4262{_}"Even if you got through to Lawrence--which is not very likely--d'you think a wheen Borderers in a fort will stay such an army?
10046{_}4263{_}It would only mean that you lost your life on the South Fork instead of in the hills, and there's little comfort in that."
10046{_}4264{_}"It's not like you to give such counsel," I said sadly.
10046{_}4265{_}"A man cannot think whether his duty will succeed as long as it's there for him to do it.
10046{_}4266{_}Maybe my news would make all the differ.
10046{_}4267{_}Maybe there would be time to get Nicholson's militia to the point of danger.
10046{_}4268{_}God has queer ways of working, if we trust Him with honest hearts.
10046{_}4269{_}Besides, a word on the Border would save the Tidewater folk, for there are ships on the James and the York to flee to if they hear in time.
10046{_}4270{_}Let Virginia go down and be delivered over to painted savages, and some day soon we will win it back; but we cannot bring life to the dead.
10046{_}4271{_}I want to save the lowland manors from what befell the D'Aubignys on the Rapidan, and if I can only do that much I will be content.
10046{_}4272{_}Will you counsel me, Ringan, to neglect my plain duty?"
10046{_}4273{_}"I gave no counsel," said Ringan hurriedly.
10046{_}4274{_}"I was only putting the common sense of it.
10046{_}4275{_}It's for you to choose."
10046{_}4276{_}Here Grey broke in.
10046{_}4277{_}"I protest against this craziness.
10046{_}4278{_}Your first duty is to your comrades and to this lady.
10046{_}4279{_}If you desert us we lose our best musket, and you have as little chance of reaching the Tidewater as the moon.
10046{_}4280{_}Arc you so madly enamoured of death, Mr.
10046{_}4281{_}Garvald?"
10046{_}4282{_}He spoke in the old stiff tones of the man I had quarrelled with.
10046{_}4283{_}I turned to Shalah.
10046{_}4284{_}"Is there any hope of getting to the South Fork?"
10046{_}4285{_}He looked me very full in the face.
10046{_}4286{_}"As much hope as a dove has who falls broken-winged into an eyrie of falcons!
10046{_}4287{_}As much hope as the deer when the hunter's knife is at its throat!
10046{_}4288{_}Yet the dove may escape, and the deer may yet tread the forest.
10046{_}4289{_}While a man draws breath there is hope, brother."
10046{_}4290{_}"Which I take to mean that the odds are a thousand against one," said Grey.
10046{_}4291{_}"Then it's my business to stake all on the one," I cried.
10046{_}4292{_}"Man, don't you see my quandary?
10046{_}4293{_}I hold a solemn trust, which I have the means of fulfilling, and I'm bound to try.
10046{_}4294{_}It's torture to me to leave you, but you will lose nothing.
10046{_}4295{_}Three men could hold this place as well as six, if the Indians are not in earnest, and, if they are, a hundred would be too few.
10046{_}4296{_}Your danger will be starvation, and I will be a mouth less to feed.
10046{_}4297{_}If I get to the Border I will find help, for we cannot stay here for ever, and how d'you think we are to get Miss Blair by ourselves to the Rappahannock with every mile littered with fighting clans?
10046{_}4298{_}I must go, or I will never have another moment's peace in life."
10046{_}4299{_}Grey was not convinced.
10046{_}4300{_}"Send the Indian," he said.
10046{_}4301{_}"And leave the stockade defenceless," I cried.
10046{_}4302{_}"It's because he stays behind that I dare to go.
10046{_}4303{_}Without him we are all bairns in the dark."
10046{_}4304{_}"That's true, anyway," said Ringan, and fell to whittling a stick.
10046{_}4305{_}"For three days," I continued, "you have food enough, and if by the end of it you are not attacked you may safely go hunting for more.
10046{_}4306{_}If nothing happens in a week's time you will know that I have failed, and you can send another messenger.
10046{_}4307{_}Ringan would be the best."
10046{_}4308{_}"That can hardly be," he said, "because I'm coming with you now."
10046{_}4309{_}I could only stare blankly.
10046{_}4310{_}"Two's better than one for this kind of business, and I am no use here--only _fruges consumere natus_, as I learned from the Inveraray dominie.
10046{_}4311{_}It's my concern as much as yours, for I brought you here, and I'm trysted with Lawrence to take back word.
10046{_}4312{_}I'm loath to leave my friends, but my place is at your side, Andrew.
10046{_}4313{_}So say no more about it."
10046{_}4314{_}I knew it was idle to protest.
10046{_}4315{_}Ringan was as obstinate as a Spanish mule when he chose, and, besides, there was reason in what he said.
10046{_}4316{_}Two were better than one both for speed in travel and for fighting if the need came, and though I had more woodcraft than he, he had ten times my wisdom.
10046{_}4317{_}There was something about his matter-of-fact tone which took the enterprise out of the land of impossibilities into a more sober realm.
10046{_}4318{_}I even began to dream of success.
10046{_}4319{_}But when.
10046{_}4320{_}I looked at Elspeth her eyes were so full of grief and care that my spirits sank again.
10046{_}4321{_}"Tell me," I cried, "that you think I am doing right, God knows it is hard to leave you, and I carry the sorest heart in Virginia.
10046{_}4322{_}But you would not have me stay idle when my plain duty commands.
10046{_}4323{_}Say that you bid me go, Elspeth."
10046{_}4324{_}"I bid you go," she said bravely, "and I will pray God to keep you safe."
10046{_}4325{_}But her eyes belied her voice, for they were swimming with tears.
10046{_}4326{_}At that moment I got the conviction that I was more to her than a mere companion, that by some miracle I had won a place in that proud and loyal heart.
10046{_}4327{_}It seemed a cruel stroke of fate that I should get this hope at the very moment when I was to leave her and go into the shadow of death.
10046{_}4328{_}But that was no hour to think of love, I took every man apart and swore him, though there was little need, to stand by the girl at all costs.
10046{_}4329{_}To Grey I opened my inmost thoughts.
10046{_}4330{_}"You and I serve one mistress," I said, "and now I confide her to your care.
10046{_}4331{_}All that I would have done I am assured you will do.
10046{_}4332{_}My heart is easier when I know that you are by her side.
10046{_}4333{_}Once we were foes, and since then we have been friends, and now you are the dearest friend on earth, for I leave you with all I cherish."
10046{_}4334{_}He flushed deeply and gave me his hand.
10046{_}4335{_}"Go in peace, sir," he said.
10046{_}4336{_}"If God wills that we perish, my last act will be to assure an easy passage to heaven for her we worship.
10046{_}4337{_}If we meet again, we meet as honourable rivals, and may that day come soon."
10046{_}4338{_}So with pistols in belt, and a supply of cartouches and some little food in our pockets, Ringan and I were enfolded in the silence of the woods.
10046{_}4339{_}CHAPTER XXIII.
10046{_}4340{_}THE HORN OF DIARMAID SOUNDS.
10046{_}4341{_}We reached the gap, and made slantwise across the farther hill.
10046{_}4342{_}I did not dare to go clown Clearwater Glen, and, besides, I was aiming for a point farther south than the Rappahannock.
10046{_}4343{_}In my wanderings with Shalah I had got a pretty good idea of the lie of the mountains on their eastern side, and I had remarked a long ridge which flung itself like a cape far into the lowlands.
10046{_}4344{_}If we could leave the hills by this, I thought we might strike the stream called the North Fork, which would bring us in time to the neighbourhood of Frew's dwelling.
10046{_}4345{_}The ridges were our only safe path, for they were thickly overgrown with woods, and the Indian bands were less likely to choose them for a route.
10046{_}4346{_}The danger was in the glens, where the trees were sparser and the broad stretches of meadow made better going for horses.
10046{_}4347{_}The movement of my legs made me pluck up heart.
10046{_}4348{_}I was embarked at any rate in a venture, and had got rid of my desperate indecision.
10046{_}4349{_}The two of us held close together, and chose the duskiest thickets, crawling belly-wise over the little clear patches and avoiding the crown of the ridge like the plague.
10046{_}4350{_}The weather helped us, for the skies hung grey and low, with wisps of vapour curling among the trees.
10046{_}4351{_}The glens were pits of mist, and my only guide was my recollection of what I had seen, and the easterly course of the streams.
10046{_}4352{_}By midday we had mounted to the crest of a long scarp which fell away in a narrow and broken promontory towards the plains.
10046{_}4353{_}So far we had seen nothing to give us pause, and the only risk lay in some Indian finding and following our trail.
10046{_}4354{_}We lay close in a scrubby wood, and rested for a little, while we ate some food.
10046{_}4355{_}Everything around us dripped with moisture, and I could have wrung pints from my coat and breeches.
10046{_}4356{_}"Oh for the Dry Tortugas!"
10046{_}4357{_}Ringan sighed.
10046{_}4358{_}"What I would give for a hot sun and the kindly winds o' the sea!
10046{_}4359{_}I thought I pined for the hills, Andrew, but I would not give a clean beach and a warm sou'-wester for all the mountains on earth."
10046{_}4360{_}Then again: "Yon's a fine lass," he would say.
10046{_}4361{_}I did not reply, for I had no heart to speak of what I had left behind.
10046{_}4362{_}"Cheer up, young one," he cried.
10046{_}4363{_}"There was more lost at Flodden.
10046{_}4364{_}A gentleman-adventurer must live by the hour, and it's surprising how Fortune favours them that trust her.
10046{_}4365{_}There was a man I mind, in Breadalbane...." And here he would tell some tale of how light came out of black darkness for the trusting heart.
10046{_}4366{_}"Man, Ringan," I said, "I see your kindly purpose.
10046{_}4367{_}But tell me, did ever you hear of such a tangle as ours being straightened out?
10046{_}4368{_}"Why, yes," he said.
10046{_}4369{_}"I've been in worse myself, and here I am.
10046{_}4370{_}I have been in a cell at Cartagena, chained to a man that had died of the plague, with the gallows preparing for me at cock-crow.
10046{_}4371{_}But in the night some friends o' mine came into the bay, and I had the solemn joy of stepping out of yon cell over the corp of the Almirante.
10046{_}4372{_}I've been mad with fever, and jumped into the Palmas River among the alligators, and not one of them touched me, though I was swimming about crying that the water was burning oil.
10046{_}4373{_}And then a lad in a boat gave me a clout on the head that knocked the daftness out of me, and in a week I was marching on my own deck, with my bonnet cocked like a king's captain.
10046{_}4374{_}I've been set by my unfriends on a rock in the Florida Keys, with a keg of dirty water and a bunch of figs, and the sun like to melt my brains, and two bullet holes in my thigh.
10046{_}4375{_}But I came out of the pickle, and lived to make the men that put me there sorry they had been born.
10046{_}4376{_}Ay, and I've seen my grave dug, and my dead clothes ready, and in a week I was making napkins out of them.
10046{_}4377{_}There's a wonderful kindness in Providence to mettled folk."
10046{_}4378{_}"Ay, Ringan, but that was only the risk of your own neck.
10046{_}4379{_}I think I could endure that.
10046{_}4380{_}But was there ever another you liked far better than yourself, that you had to see in deadly peril?"
10046{_}4381{_}"No.
10046{_}4382{_}I'll be honest with you, there never was.
10046{_}4383{_}I grant you that's the hardest thing to thole.
10046{_}4384{_}But you'll keep a stiff lip even to that, seeing you are the braver of the two of us."
10046{_}4385{_}At that I cried out in expostulation, but Ringan was firm.
10046{_}4386{_}"Ay, the braver by far, and I'll say it again.
10046{_}4387{_}I'm a man of the dancing blood, with a rare appetite for frays and forays.
10046{_}4388{_}You are the sedate soul that would be happier at home in the chimney corner.
10046{_}4389{_}And yet you are the most determined of the lot of us, though you have no pleasure in it.
10046{_}4390{_}Why?
10046{_}4391{_}Just because you are the bravest.
10046{_}4392{_}You can force yourself to a job when flesh and spirit cry out against it.
10046{_}4393{_}I let no man alive cry down my courage, but I say freely that it's not to be evened with yours."
10046{_}4394{_}I was not feeling very courageous.
10046{_}4395{_}As we sped along the ridge in the afternoon I seemed to myself like a midge lost in a monstrous net.
10046{_}4396{_}The dank, dripping trees and the misty hills seemed to muffle and deaden the world.
10046{_}4397{_}I could not believe that they ever would end; that anywhere there was a clear sky and open country.
10046{_}4398{_}And I had always the feeling that in those banks of vapour lurked deadly enemies who any moment might steal out and encompass us.
10046{_}4399{_}But about four o'clock the weather lightened, and from the cock's-comb on which we moved we looked down into the lower glens.
10046{_}4400{_}I saw that we had left the main flanks of the range behind us, and were now fairly on a cape which jutted out beyond the other ridges.
10046{_}4401{_}It behoved us now to go warily, and where the thickets grew thin we moved like hunters, in every hollow and crack that could shelter a man.
10046{_}4402{_}Ringan led, and led well, for he had not stalked the red deer on the braes of Breadalbane for nothing.
10046{_}4403{_}But no sign of life appeared in the green hollows on either hand, neither in the meadow spaces nor by the creeks of the growing streams.
10046{_}4404{_}The world was dead silent; not even a bird showed in the whole firmament.
10046{_}4405{_}Lower and lower we went, till the end of the ridge was before us, a slope which melted into the river plains.
10046{_}4406{_}A single shaft of bright sunshine broke from the clouds behind us, and showed the tumbled country of low downs and shallow vales which stretched to the Tidewater border.
10046{_}4407{_}I had a momentary gleam of hope, as sudden and transient as that ray of light.
10046{_}4408{_}We were almost out of the hills, and, that accomplished, we were most likely free of the Indian forces that gathered there.
10046{_}4409{_}I had come to share the Rappahannock men's opinion about the Cherokees.
10046{_}4410{_}If we could escape the strange tribes from the west, I looked for no trouble at the hands of those common raiders.
10046{_}4411{_}The thicket ended with the ridge, and there was a quarter-mile of broken meadow before the forest began.
10046{_}4412{_}It was a queer place, that patch of green grass set like an arena for an audience on the mountain side.
10046{_}4413{_}A fine stream ran through it, coming down the glen on our right, and falling afterwards into a dark, woody ravine.
10046{_}4414{_}I mistrusted the look of it, for there was no cover, and 'twas in full view of the whole flanks of the hills.
10046{_}4415{_}Ringan, too, was disturbed.
10046{_}4416{_}"Twould be wiser like to wait for darkness before trying that bit," he said.
10046{_}4417{_}"We'll be terrible kenspeckle to the gentry we ken of."
10046{_}4418{_}But I would not hear of delay.
10046{_}4419{_}Now that we were all but out of the hills I was mad to get forward.
10046{_}4420{_}I thought foolishly that every minute we delayed there we increased our peril, and I longed for the covering of the lowland forest.
10046{_}4421{_}Besides, I thought that by using some of the crinkles in the meadow we could be sheltered from any eyes on the slopes.
10046{_}4422{_}Ringan poked his head out of the covert and took a long gaze.
10046{_}4423{_}"The place seems empty enough, but I cannot like it.
10046{_}4424{_}Have you your pistols handy, Andrew?
10046{_}4425{_}I see what looks like an Indian track, and if we were to meet a brave or two, it would be a pity to let them betray us."
10046{_}4426{_}I looked at my pistols to see if the damp woods had spoiled the priming.
10046{_}4427{_}"Well, here's for fortune," said Ringan, and we scrambled off the ridge, and plunged into the lush grasses of the meadow.
10046{_}4428{_}Had we kept our heads and crossed as prudently as we had made the morning's journey, all might have been well.
10046{_}4429{_}But a madcap haste seemed to possess us.
10046{_}4430{_}We tore through the herbage as if we had been running a race in the yard of a peaceful manor.
10046{_}4431{_}The stream stayed us a little, for it could not be forded without a wetting, and I went in up to the waist.
10046{_}4432{_}As we scrambled up the far bank some impulse made me turn my head.
10046{_}4433{_}There, coming down the water, was a band of Indians.
10046{_}4434{_}They were still some distance off, but they saw us, and put their horses to the gallop.
10046{_}4435{_}I cried to Ringan to run for the shelter of the woods, for in the open we were at their mercy.
10046{_}4436{_}He cast one glance over his shoulder, and set a pace which came near to foundering me.
10046{_}4437{_}We got what we wanted earlier than we had hoped.
10046{_}4438{_}The woods in front rose in a high bluff, and down a little ravine a burn trickled.
10046{_}4439{_}The sides were too steep and matted for horses to travel, and he who stood in the ravine had his back and flanks defended.
10046{_}4440{_}"Now for a fight, Andrew lad," cried Ringan, his eyes dancing.
10046{_}4441{_}"Stick you to the pistols, and I'll show them something in the way of sword-play."
10046{_}4442{_}The Indians wheeled up to the edge of the ravine, and I saw to my joy that they did not carry bows.
10046{_}4443{_}One had a musket, but it looked as if he had no powder left, for it swung idly on his back.
10046{_}4444{_}They had tomahawks at their belts and long shining knives with deerhorn handles.
10046{_}4445{_}I only got a glimpse of them, but 'twas enough to show me they were of that Western nation that I dreaded.
10046{_}4446{_}They were gone in an instant.
10046{_}4447{_}"That looks bad for us, Andrew," Ringan said.
10046{_}4448{_}"If they had come down on us yelling for our scalps, we would have had a merry meeting.
10046{_}4449{_}But they're either gone to bring their friends or they're trying to take us in the back.
10046{_}4450{_}I'll guard the front, and you keep your eyes on the hinder parts, though a jackdaw could scarcely win over these craigs."
10046{_}4451{_}A sudden burst of sun came out, while Ringan and I waited uneasily.
10046{_}4452{_}The great blue roll of mountain we had left was lit below the mist with a glory of emerald and gold.
10046{_}4453{_}Ringan was whistling softly through his teeth, while I scanned the half moon of rock and matted vines which made our shelter.
10046{_}4454{_}There was no sound in the air but the tap of a woodpecker and the trickling of the little runlets from the wet sides.
10046{_}4455{_}The mind in a close watch falls under a spell, so that while the senses are alert the thoughts are apt to wander.
10046{_}4456{_}As I have said before, I have the sharpest sight, and as I watched a point of rock it seemed to move ever so slightly.
10046{_}4457{_}I rubbed my eyes and thought it fancy, and a sudden noise above made me turn my head.
10046{_}4458{_}It was only a bird, and as I looked again at the rock it seemed as if a spray of vine had blown athwart it, which was not there before.
10046{_}4459{_}I gazed intently, and, following the spray into the shadow, I saw something liquid and mottled like a toad's skin.
10046{_}4460{_}As I stared it flickered and shimmered.
10046{_}4461{_}'Twas only the light on a wet leaf, I told myself; but surely it had not been there before.
10046{_}4462{_}A sudden suspicion seized me, and I lifted my pistol and fired.
10046{_}4463{_}There was a shudder in the thicket, and an Indian, shot through the head, rolled into the burn.
10046{_}4464{_}At the sound I heard Ringan cry out, and there came a great war-whoop from the mouth of the ravine.
10046{_}4465{_}I gave one look, and then turned to my own business, for as the dead man fell another leaped from the matted cliffs.
10046{_}4466{_}My second pistol missed fire.
10046{_}4467{_}In crossing the stream I must have damped the priming.
10046{_}4468{_}What happened next is all confusion in my mind.
10046{_}4469{_}I dodged the fall of the knife, and struck hard with my pistol butt at the uplifted arm.
10046{_}4470{_}I felt no fear, only intense anger at my folly in not having looked better to my priming.
10046{_}4471{_}But the shock of the man's charge upset me, and the next I knew of it we were wrestling on the ground.
10046{_}4472{_}I had his right arm by the wrist, but I was no match for him in suppleness, and in the position in which we lay I could not use the weight of my shoulders.
10046{_}4473{_}The most I could do was to keep him from striking, and to effect that my strength was stretched to its uttermost.
10046{_}4474{_}My eyes filmed with weariness, and my breath came in gasps, for, remember, I had been up all night, and that day had already travelled many miles.
10046{_}4475{_}I remember yet the sickly smell of his greasy skin and the red hate of his eyes.
10046{_}4476{_}As we struggled I could see Ringan holding the mouth of the ravine with his sword.
10046{_}4477{_}One of his foes he had shot, and the best blade in the Five Seas was now engaged with three Indian knives.
10046{_}4478{_}I heard his happy whistling, and a grunt now and then from a wounded foe.
10046{_}4479{_}He had enough to do, and could give me no aid.
10046{_}4480{_}And as I realized this I felt the grip of my arms growing slacker, and knew that in a second or two I should feel that long Indian steel.
10046{_}4481{_}I made a desperate effort, and swung round so that I got my left shoulder on his knife arm.
10046{_}4482{_}That brought my right shoulder close to his mouth, and he bit me to the bone.
10046{_}4483{_}The wound did me good, for it maddened me, and I got a knee loose, and forced it into his loins.
10046{_}4484{_}For a moment I dreamed of victory, but I had not counted on the wiles of a savage.
10046{_}4485{_}He lay quite limp for a second, and, as I relaxed my effort a little, seized the occasion to slip from beneath me and let me roll into the burn.
10046{_}4486{_}The next instant he was above me, and I saw the knife against the sky.
10046{_}4487{_}I thought that all was over.
10046{_}4488{_}He pushed back his hair from his eyes, and the steel quivered.
10046{_}4489{_}And then something thrust between me and the point, there was a leap and a shudder, and I was gazing at emptiness.
10046{_}4490{_}I lay gazing, for I seemed bereft of wits.
10046{_}4491{_}Then a voice cried, "Are you hurt, Andrew?"
10046{_}4492{_}and I got to my feet.
10046{_}4493{_}My enemy lay in the pool of the burn, with a hole through his throat from Ringan's sword.
10046{_}4494{_}A little farther off lay the savage I had shot.
10046{_}4495{_}At the mouth of the ravine lay three dead Indians.
10046{_}4496{_}The last of the six must have fled.
10046{_}4497{_}Ringan had sheathed his blade, and was looking at me with a queer smile on his face.
10046{_}4498{_}"Yon was a merry bout, Andrew," he said, and his voice sounded very far away.
10046{_}4499{_}Then he swayed into my arms, and I saw that his vest was dark with blood.
10046{_}4500{_}"What is it?"
10046{_}4501{_}I cried in wild fear.
10046{_}4502{_}"Are you hurt, Ringan?"
10046{_}4503{_}I laid him on a bed of moss, and opened his shirt.
10046{_}4504{_}In his breast was a gaping wound from which the bright blood was welling.
10046{_}4505{_}He lay with his eyes closed while I strove to stanch the flow.
10046{_}4506{_}Then he choked, and as I raised his head there came a gush of blood from his lips.
10046{_}4507{_}"That man of yours...." he whispered.
10046{_}4508{_}"I got his knife before he got my sword....
10046{_}4509{_}I doubt it went deep...."  "O Ringan," I cried, "it's me that's to blame.
10046{_}4510{_}You got it trying to save me.
10046{_}4511{_}You're not going to leave me, Ringan?"
10046{_}4512{_}He was easier now, and the first torrent of blood had subsided.
10046{_}4513{_}But his breath laboured, and there was pain in his eyes.
10046{_}4514{_}"I've got my call," he said faintly.
10046{_}4515{_}"Who would have thought that Ninian Campbell would meet his death from an Indian shabble?
10046{_}4516{_}They'll no believe it at Tortuga.
10046{_}4517{_}Still and on...."  I brought him water in my hat, and for a moment he breathed freely.
10046{_}4518{_}He motioned me to put my ear close.
10046{_}4519{_}"You'll send word to the folk in Breadalbane.... Just say that I came by an honest end....
10046{_}4520{_}Cheer up, lad.
10046{_}4521{_}You'll live to see happy days yet....
10046{_}4522{_}But keep mind of me, Andrew.... Man, I liked you well, and would have been blithe to keep you company a bit longer...."  I was crying like a child.
10046{_}4523{_}There was a little gold charm on a cord round his neck, now dyed with his blood.
10046{_}4524{_}He motioned me to look at it.
10046{_}4525{_}"Give it to the lass," he whispered.
10046{_}4526{_}"I had once a lass like yon, and I aye wore it for her sake.
10046{_}4527{_}I've had a roving life, with many ill deeds in it, but doubtless the Almighty will make allowances.
10046{_}4528{_}Can you say a bit prayer, Andrew?"
10046{_}4529{_}As well as I could, I repeated that Psalm I had said over the graves by the Rapidan.
10046{_}4530{_}He looked at me with eyes as clear and honest as a child's.
10046{_}4531{_}"'In death's dark vale I will fear no ill,'" he repeated after me.
10046{_}4532{_}"That minds me of lang syne.
10046{_}4533{_}I never feared muckle on earth, and I'll not begin now."
10046{_}4534{_}I saw that the end was very near.
10046{_}4535{_}The pain had gone, and there was a queer innocence in his lean face.
10046{_}4536{_}His eyes shut and opened again, and each time the light was dimmer.
10046{_}4537{_}Suddenly he lifted himself.
10046{_}4538{_}"The Horn of Diarmaid has sounded," he cried, and dropped back in my arms.
10046{_}4539{_}That was the last word he spoke.
10046{_}4540{_}I watched by him till the dark fell, and long after.
10046{_}4541{_}Then as the moon rose I bestirred myself, and looked for a place of burial.
10046{_}4542{_}I would not have him lie in that narrow ravine, so I carried him into the meadow, and found a hole which some wild beast had deserted.
10046{_}4543{_}Painfully and slowly with my knife I made it into a shallow grave, where I laid him, with some boulders above.
10046{_}4544{_}Then I think I flung myself on the earth and wept my fill.
10046{_}4545{_}I had lost my best of friends, and the ache of regret and loneliness was too bitter to bear.
10046{_}4546{_}I asked for nothing better than to join him soon on the other side.
10046{_}4547{_}After a while I forced myself to rise.
10046{_}4548{_}He had praised my courage that very day, and if I was to be true to him I must be true to my trust.
10046{_}4549{_}I told myself that Ringan would never have countenanced this idle grief.
10046{_}4550{_}I girt on his sword, and hung the gold charm round my neck.
10046{_}4551{_}Then I took my bearings as well as I could, re-loaded my pistols, and marched into the woods, keeping to the course of the little river.
10046{_}4552{_}As I went I remember that always a little ahead I seemed to hear the merry lilt of Ringan's whistling.
10046{_}4553{_}CHAPTER XXIV.
10046{_}4554{_}I SUFFER THE HEATHEN'S RAGE  As I stumbled through the moonlit forest I heard Ringan's tunes ever crooning among the trees.
10046{_}4555{_}First it was the old mad march of "Bundle and go," which the pipers play when the clans are rising.
10046{_}4556{_}Then it changed to the lilt of "Colin's Cattle," which is an air that the fairies made, and sung in the ear of a shepherd who fell asleep in one of their holy places.
10046{_}4557{_}And then it lost all mortal form, and became a thing as faint as the wind in the tree-tops or the humming of bees in clover.
10046{_}4558{_}My weary legs stepped out to this wizard music, and the spell of it lulled my fevered thoughts into the dull patience of the desperate.
10046{_}4559{_}At an open space where I could see the sky I tried to take further bearings.
10046{_}4560{_}I must move south-east by east, and in time I must come to Lawrence.
10046{_}4561{_}I do not think I had any hope of getting there, for I knew that long ere this the man who escaped must have returned with others, and that now they would be hot on my trail.
10046{_}4562{_}What could one lad do in a wide woodland against the cunningest trackers on earth?
10046{_}4563{_}But Ringan had praised my courage, and I could not fail him.
10046{_}4564{_}I should go on till I died, and I did not think that would be very long.
10046{_}4565{_}My pistols, re-loaded, pressed against my side, and Ringan's sword swung by my thigh.
10046{_}4566{_}I was determined to make a good ending, since that was all now left to me.
10046{_}4567{_}In that hour I had forgotten about everything--about the peril of Virginia, even about Elspeth and the others in the fort on the hill-top.
10046{_}4568{_}There comes a time to every one when the world narrows for him to a strait alley, with Death at the end of it, and all his thoughts are fixed on that waiting enemy of mankind.
10046{_}4569{_}My senses were blunted, and I took no note of the noises of the forest.
10046{_}4570{_}As I passed down a ravine a stone dropped behind me, but I did not pause to wonder why.
10046{_}4571{_}A twig crackled on my left, but it did not disquiet me, and there was a rustling in the thicket which was not the breeze.
10046{_}4572{_}I marked nothing, as I plodded on with vacant mind and eye.
10046{_}4573{_}So when I tripped on a vine and fell, I was scarcely surprised when I found I could not rise.
10046{_}4574{_}Men had sprung up silently around me, and I was pinned by many hands.
10046{_}4575{_}They trussed me with ropes, binding my hands cruelly behind my back, and swathing my legs till not a muscle could move.
10046{_}4576{_}My pistols hung idle, and the ropes drove the hafts into my flesh.
10046{_}4577{_}This is the end, thought I, and I did not even grieve at my impotence.
10046{_}4578{_}My courage now was of the passive kind, not to act but to endure.
10046{_}4579{_}Always I kept telling myself that I must be brave, for Ringan had praised my courage, and I had a conviction that nothing that man could do would shake me.
10046{_}4580{_}Thanks be to God, my quick fancy was dulled, and I did not try to look into the future.
10046{_}4581{_}I lived for the moment, and I was resolved that the moment should find me unmoved.
10046{_}4582{_}They carried me to where their horses were tied up in a glade, and presently we were galloping towards the hills, myself an inert bundle strapped across an Indian saddle.
10046{_}4583{_}The pain of the motion was great, but I had a kind of grim comfort in bearing it.
10046{_}4584{_}After a time I think my senses left me, and I slipped into a stupor, from which I woke with a fiery ache at every joint and eyes distended with a blinding heat.
10046{_}4585{_}Some one tossed me on the ground, where I lay with my cheek in a cool, wet patch of earth.
10046{_}4586{_}Then I felt my bonds being unloosed, and a strong arm pulled me to my feet.
10046{_}4587{_}When it let go I dropped again, and not till many hands had raised me and set me on a log could I look round at my whereabouts.
10046{_}4588{_}I was in a crook of a hill glen, lit with a great radiance of moonlight.
10046{_}4589{_}Fires dotted the flat, and Indian tents, and there seemed to me hundreds of savages crowding in on me.
10046{_}4590{_}I do not suppose that I showed any fear, for my bodily weakness had made me as impassive as any Indian.
10046{_}4591{_}Presently a voice spoke to me, but I could not understand the words.
10046{_}4592{_}I shook my head feebly, and another spoke.
10046{_}4593{_}This time I knew that the tongue was Cherokee, a speech I could recognize but could not follow.
10046{_}4594{_}Again I shook my head, and a third took up the parable.
10046{_}4595{_}This one spoke the Powhatan language, which I knew, and I replied in the same tongue.
10046{_}4596{_}There was a tall man wearing in his hair a single great feather, whom I took to be the chief.
10046{_}4597{_}He spoke to me through the interpreter, and asked me whence I came.
10046{_}4598{_}I told him I was a hunter who had strayed in the hills.
10046{_}4599{_}He asked where the other was.
10046{_}4600{_}"He is dead," I said, "dead of your knives.
10046{_}4601{_}But five of your braves atoned for him."
10046{_}4602{_}"You speak truth," he said gravely.
10046{_}4603{_}"But the Children of the West Wind do not suffer the death of, their sons to go unrewarded.
10046{_}4604{_}For each one of the five, three Palefaces shall eat the dust in the day of our triumph."
10046{_}4605{_}"Be it so," said I stoutly, though I felt a dreadful nausea coming over me.
10046{_}4606{_}I was determined to keep my head high, if only my frail body would not fail me.
10046{_}4607{_}"The Sons of the West Wind," he spoke again, "have need of warriors.
10046{_}4608{_}You can atone for the slaughter you have caused, and the blood feud will be forgotten.
10046{_}4609{_}In the space of five suns we shall sweep the Palefaces into the sea, and rule all the land to the Eastern waters.
10046{_}4610{_}My brother is a man of his hands, and valour is dear to the heart of Onotawah.
10046{_}4611{_}If he casts in his lot with the Children of the West Wind a wigwam shall be his, and a daughter of our race to wife, and six of our young men shall follow his commands.
10046{_}4612{_}Will my brother march with us against those whom God has delivered to us for our prey?"
10046{_}4613{_}"Does the eagle make terms with the kite?"
10046{_}4614{_}I asked, "and fly with them to raid his own eyrie?
10046{_}4615{_}Yes, I will join with you, and march with you till I have delivered you to, perhaps, a score of the warriors of my own people.
10046{_}4616{_}Then I will aid them in making carrion of you."
10046{_}4617{_}Heaven knows what wrought on me to speak like this, I, a poor, broken fellow, face to face with a hundred men-at-arms.
10046{_}4618{_}I think my mind had forsaken me altogether, and I spoke like a drunken man with a tongue not my own.
10046{_}4619{_}I had only the one idea in my foolish head--to be true to Ringan, and to meet the death of which I was assured with an unflinching face.
10046{_}4620{_}Yet perhaps my very madness was the course of discretion.
10046{_}4621{_}You cannot move an Indian by pity, and he will show mercy only to one who, like a gamecock, asks nothing less.
10046{_}4622{_}The chief heard me gravely, and spoke to the others.
10046{_}4623{_}One cried out something in a savage voice, and for a moment a fierce argument was raised, which the chief settled with uplifted hand.
10046{_}4624{_}"My brother speaks bold words," he said.
10046{_}4625{_}"The spirits of his fathers cry out for the companionship of such a hero.
10046{_}4626{_}When the wrongs of our race have been avenged, I wish him good hunting in the Kingdom of the Sunset."
10046{_}4627{_}They took me and stripped me mother naked.
10046{_}4628{_}Has any man who reads this tale ever faced an enemy in his bare feet?
10046{_}4629{_}If so, he will know that the heart of man is more in his boots than philosophers wot of.
10046{_}4630{_}Without them he feels lost and unprepared, and the edge gone from his spirit.
10046{_}4631{_}But without his clothes he is in a far worse case.
10046{_}4632{_}The winds of heaven play round his nakedness; every thorn and twig is his assailant, and the whole of him seems a mark for the arrows of his foes.
10046{_}4633{_}That stripping was the thing that brought me to my senses.
10046{_}4634{_}I recognized that I was to be the subject of those hellish tortures which the Indians use, the tales of which are on every Borderer's lips.
10046{_}4635{_}And yet I did not recognize it fully, or my courage must have left me then and there.
10046{_}4636{_}My imagination was still limping, and I foresaw only a death of pain, not the horrid incidents of its preparation.
10046{_}4637{_}Death I could face, and I summoned up every shred of my courage.
10046{_}4638{_}Ringan's voice was still in my ear, his airy songs still sang themselves in my brain.
10046{_}4639{_}I would not shame him, but oh!
10046{_}4640{_}how I envied him lying, all troubles past, in his quiet grave!
10046{_}4641{_}The night was mild, and the yellow radiance of the moon seemed almost warmth-giving.
10046{_}4642{_}I sat on that log in a sort of stupor, watching my enemies preparing my entertainment.
10046{_}4643{_}One thing I noted, that there were no women in the camp.
10046{_}4644{_}I remembered that I had heard that the most devilish tortures were those which the squaws devised, and that the Indian men were apt to be quicker and more merciful in their murderings.
10046{_}4645{_}Then I was lifted up and carried to a flat space beside the stream, where the trunk of a young pine had been set upright in the ground.
10046{_}4646{_}A man, waving a knife, and singing a wild song, danced towards me.
10046{_}4647{_}He seized me by the hair, and I actually rejoiced, for I knew that the pain of scalping would make me oblivious of all else.
10046{_}4648{_}But he only drew the sharp point of the knife in a circle round my head, scarce breaking the skin.
10046{_}4649{_}I had grace given me to keep a stout face, mainly because I was relieved that this was to be my fate.
10046{_}4650{_}He put the knife back in his girdle, and others laid hold on me.
10046{_}4651{_}They smeared my lower limbs with some kind of grease which smelt of resin.
10046{_}4652{_}One savage who had picked up a brand from one of the little fires dropped some of the stuff on it, and it crackled merrily.
10046{_}4653{_}He grinned at me--a slow, diabolical grin.
10046{_}4654{_}They lashed me to the stake with ropes of green vine.
10046{_}4655{_}Then they piled dry hay a foot deep around me, and laid above it wood and green branches.
10046{_}4656{_}To make the fuel still greener, they poured water on it.
10046{_}4657{_}At the moment I did not see the object of these preparations, but now I can understand it.
10046{_}4658{_}The dry hay would serve to burn my legs, which had already been anointed with the inflammable grease.
10046{_}4659{_}So I should suffer a gradual torture, for it would be long ere the flames reached a vital part.
10046{_}4660{_}I think they erred, for they assumed that I had the body of an Indian, which does not perish till a blow is struck at its heart; whereas I am confident that any white man would be dead of the anguish long ere the fire had passed beyond his knees.
10046{_}4661{_}I think that was the most awful moment of my life.
10046{_}4662{_}Indeed I could not have endured it had not my mind been drugged and my body stupid with fatigue.
10046{_}4663{_}Men have often asked me what were my thoughts in that hour, while the faggots were laid about my feet.
10046{_}4664{_}I cannot tell, for I have no very clear memory.
10046{_}4665{_}The Power which does not break the bruised reed tempered the storm to my frailty.
10046{_}4666{_}I could not envisage the future, and so was mercifully enabled to look only to the moment.
10046{_}4667{_}I knew that pain was coming; but I was already in pain, and the sick man does not trouble himself about degrees of suffering.
10046{_}4668{_}Death, too, was coming; but for that I had been long ready.
10046{_}4669{_}The hardest thing that man can do is to endure, but this was to me no passive endurance; it was an active struggle to show a fortitude worthy of the gallant dead.
10046{_}4670{_}So I must suppose that I hung there in my bonds with a motionless face and a mouth which gave out no cry.
10046{_}4671{_}They brought the faggots, and poured on water, and I did not look their way.
10046{_}4672{_}Some score of braves began a war dance, circling round me, waving their tomahawks, and singing their wild chants.
10046{_}4673{_}For me they did not break the moonlit silence, I was hearing other sounds and seeing far other sights.
10046{_}4674{_}An old sad song of Ringan's was in my ears, something about an exile who cried out in France for the red heather and the salt winds of the Isles.
